<li><a href="/wiki/Rotational_speed" title="Rotational speed">Rotational speed</a></li></ul>
</div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Angular_acceleration" title="Angular acceleration">Angular acceleration</a> / <a href="/wiki/Angular_displacement" title="Angular displacement">displacement</a> / <a href="/wiki/Angular_frequency" title="Angular frequency">frequency</a> / <a href="/wiki/Angular_velocity" title="Angular velocity">velocity</a></li></ul></div></div></td>

<li><span class="nowrap"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/16px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/23px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/31px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Category:Classical_mechanics" title="Category:Classical mechanics">Category</a></span></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Classical_mechanics" title="Template:Classical mechanics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Classical_mechanics" title="Template talk:Classical mechanics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Classical_mechanics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In physics, <b>gravity</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <i> gravitas</i> 'weight'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>) is a <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">fundamental interaction</a> which causes mutual attraction between all things with <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> or <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>. Gravity is, by far, the weakest of the four fundamental interactions, approximately 10<sup>38</sup> times weaker than the <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong interaction</a>, 10<sup>36</sup> times weaker than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">electromagnetic force</a> and 10<sup>29</sup> times weaker than the <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak interaction</a>. As a result, it has no significant influence at the level of <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> However, gravity is the most significant interaction between objects at the <a href="/wiki/Macroscopic_scale" title="Macroscopic scale">macroscopic scale</a>, and it determines the motion of <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a>, and even <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">light</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">On Earth</a>, gravity gives <a href="/wiki/Weight" title="Weight">weight</a> to <a href="/wiki/Physical_object" title="Physical object">physical objects</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Gravitation_of_the_Moon" title="Gravitation of the Moon">Moon's gravity</a> is responsible for sublunar <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a> in the oceans (the corresponding antipodal tide is caused by the inertia of the Earth and Moon orbiting one another). Gravity also has many important biological functions, helping to guide the growth of plants through the process of <a href="/wiki/Gravitropism" title="Gravitropism">gravitropism</a> and influencing the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulation</a> of fluids in <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular organisms</a>. Investigation into the effects of <a href="/wiki/Weightlessness" title="Weightlessness">weightlessness</a> has shown that gravity may play a role in <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> function and <a href="/wiki/Cellular_differentiation" title="Cellular differentiation">cell differentiation</a> within the human body.
</p><p>The gravitational attraction between the original gaseous matter in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a> allowed it to <a href="/wiki/Coalescence_(physics)" title="Coalescence (physics)">coalesce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">form stars</a> which eventually condensed into galaxies, so gravity is responsible for many of the large-scale structures in the Universe. Gravity has an infinite range, although its effects become weaker as objects get farther away.
</p><p>Gravity is most accurately described by the <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general theory of relativity</a> (proposed by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> in 1915), which describes gravity not as a force, but as the <a href="/wiki/Curvature" title="Curvature">curvature</a> of <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a>, caused by the uneven distribution of mass, and causing masses to move along <a href="/wiki/Geodesic" title="Geodesic">geodesic</a> lines. The most extreme example of this curvature of spacetime is a <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a>, from which nothing—not even light—can escape once past the black hole's <a href="/wiki/Event_horizon" title="Event horizon">event horizon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> However, for most applications, gravity is well approximated by <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton's law of universal gravitation">Newton's law of universal gravitation</a>, which describes gravity as a <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a> causing any two bodies to be attracted toward each other, with magnitude <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics)" title="Proportionality (mathematics)">proportional</a> to the product of their masses and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inversely_proportional" title="Inversely proportional">inversely proportional</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Square_(algebra)" title="Square (algebra)">square</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Distance" title="Distance">distance</a> between them:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F=G{\frac {m_{1}m_{2}}{r^{2}}},}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>G</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msub>
<mi>m</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F=G{\frac {m_{1}m_{2}}{r^{2}}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle F=G{\frac {m_{1}m_{2}}{r^{2}}},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/48f74b3b4d591ba1996c4d481f74ac3ab7e279d7" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:14.338ex; height:5.009ex;"/></div>where <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">F</span> is the force, <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i><sub>1</sub></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i><sub>2</sub></span> are the masses of the objects interacting, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">r</span> is the distance between the centers of the masses and <span class="texhtml"><i>G</i></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">gravitational constant</a>.
</p><p>Current models of <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a> imply that the earliest instance of gravity in the Universe, possibly in the form of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">quantum gravity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supergravity" title="Supergravity">supergravity</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_singularity" title="Gravitational singularity">gravitational singularity</a>, along with ordinary <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> and <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a>, developed during the <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_universe#Planck_epoch" title="Chronology of the universe">Planck epoch</a> (up to 10<sup>−43</sup> seconds after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">birth</a> of the Universe), possibly from a primeval state, such as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/False_vacuum" title="False vacuum">false vacuum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_vacuum" title="Quantum vacuum">quantum vacuum</a> or <a href="/wiki/Virtual_particle" title="Virtual particle">virtual particle</a>, in a currently unknown manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck-UOregon_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planck-UOregon-4">[4]</a></sup> Scientists are currently working to develop a theory of gravity consistent with <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">quantum gravity</a> theory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20221010_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20221010-5">[5]</a></sup> which would allow gravity to be united in a common mathematical framework (a <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_everything" title="Theory of everything">theory of everything</a>) with the other three fundamental interactions of physics.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_gravitational_theory" title="History of gravitational theory">History of gravitational theory</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_world">Ancient world</span></h3>
<p>The nature and mechanism of gravity was explored by a wide range of ancient scholars. In <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> believed that objects fell towards the Earth because the Earth was the center of the Universe and attracted all of the mass in the Universe towards it. He also thought that the speed of a falling object should increase with its weight, a conclusion which was later shown to be false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> While Aristotle's view was widely accepted throughout Ancient Greece, there were other thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> who correctly predicted that the attraction of gravity was not unique to the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although he didn't understand gravity as a force, the ancient Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> discovered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_of_gravity" title="Center of gravity">center of gravity</a> of a triangle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> He also postulated that if two equal weights did not have the same center of gravity, the center of gravity of the two weights together would be in the middle of the line that joins their centers of gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, the mathematician-astronomer <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a> first identified gravity to explain why objects are not driven away from the Earth by the <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force" title="Centrifugal force">centrifugal force</a> of <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth's rotation">the planet's rotation</a>. Later, in the seventh century CE, <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a> proposed the idea that gravity is an attractive force which draws objects to the Earth and used the term <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E0%A4%97%E0%A5%81%E0%A4%B0%E0%A5%81%E0%A4%A4%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%B5%E0%A4%BE%E0%A4%95%E0%A4%B0%E0%A5%8D%E0%A4%B7%E0%A4%A3" title="wikt:गुरुत्वाकर्षण">gurutvākarṣaṇ</a></i> to describe it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the ancient <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, gravity was a topic of fierce debate. The <a href="/wiki/Persians" title="Persians">Persian</a> intellectual <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a> believed that the force of gravity was not unique to the Earth, and he correctly assumed that other <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">heavenly bodies</a> should exert a gravitational attraction as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Al-Khazini" title="Al-Khazini">Al-Khazini</a> held the same position as Aristotle that all matter in the Universe is attracted to the center of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa_SB.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3875" data-file-width="2544" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/The_Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa_SB.jpeg/180px-The_Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa_SB.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/The_Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa_SB.jpeg/270px-The_Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa_SB.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/The_Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa_SB.jpeg/360px-The_Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa_SB.jpeg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa_SB.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa" title="Leaning Tower of Pisa">Leaning Tower of Pisa</a>, where according to legend Galileo performed an experiment about the speed of falling objects</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_revolution">Scientific revolution</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" title="Scientific revolution">Scientific revolution</a></div>
<p>In the mid-16th century, various European scientists experimentally disproved the <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_physics" title="Aristotelian physics">Aristotelian</a> notion that heavier objects <a href="/wiki/Free_fall" title="Free fall">fall</a> at a faster rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-15">[15]</a></sup> In particular, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_people" title="Spanish people">Spanish</a> <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican</a> priest <a href="/wiki/Domingo_de_Soto" title="Domingo de Soto">Domingo de Soto</a> wrote in 1551 that bodies in <a href="/wiki/Free_fall" title="Free fall">free fall</a> uniformly accelerate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-15">[15]</a></sup> De Soto may have been influenced by earlier experiments conducted by other Dominican priests in Italy, including those by <a href="/wiki/Benedetto_Varchi" title="Benedetto Varchi">Benedetto Varchi</a>, Francesco Beato, <a href="/wiki/Luca_Ghini" title="Luca Ghini">Luca Ghini</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Giovan_Battista_Bellaso" title="Giovan Battista Bellaso">Giovan Bellaso</a> which contradicted Aristotle's teachings on the fall of bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-15">[15]</a></sup> The mid-16th century Italian physicist <a href="/wiki/Giambattista_Benedetti" title="Giambattista Benedetti">Giambattista Benedetti</a> published papers claiming that, due to <a href="/wiki/Relative_density" title="Relative density">specific gravity</a>, objects made of the same material but with different masses would fall at the same speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drabkin_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drabkin-16">[16]</a></sup> With the 1586 <a href="/wiki/Delft_tower_experiment" title="Delft tower experiment">Delft tower experiment</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Flemish" title="Flemish">Flemish</a> physicist <a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a> observed that two cannonballs of differing sizes and weights fell at the same rate when dropped from a tower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stevin_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stevin-17">[17]</a></sup> Finally, in the late 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>'s careful measurements of balls rolling down <a href="/wiki/Inclined_plane" title="Inclined plane">inclines</a> allowed him to firmly establish that gravitational acceleration is the same for all objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Galileo postulated that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_resistance" title="Air resistance">air resistance</a> is the reason that objects with a low density and high <a href="/wiki/Surface_area" title="Surface area">surface area</a> fall more slowly in an atmosphere. 
</p><p>In 1604, Galileo correctly hypothesized that the distance of a falling object is proportional to the <a href="/wiki/Square_(algebra)" title="Square (algebra)">square</a> of the time elapsed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> This was later confirmed by Italian scientists <a href="/wiki/Jesuits" title="Jesuits">Jesuits</a> <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Maria_Grimaldi" title="Francesco Maria Grimaldi">Grimaldi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Riccioli" title="Giovanni Battista Riccioli">Riccioli</a> between 1640 and 1650. They also calculated the magnitude of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_gravity" title="Earth's gravity">the Earth's gravity</a> by measuring the oscillations of a pendulum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Newton.27s_theory_of_gravitation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Newton's_theory_of_gravitation">Newton's theory of gravitation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton's law of universal gravitation">Newton's law of universal gravitation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton,_1689.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2671" data-file-width="2218" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton%2C_1689.jpg/200px-Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton%2C_1689.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton%2C_1689.jpg/300px-Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton%2C_1689.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton%2C_1689.jpg/400px-Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton%2C_1689.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton,_1689.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>English physicist and mathematician, Sir <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> (1642–1727)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1684, Newton sent a manuscript to <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">Edmond Halley</a> titled <i><a href="/wiki/De_motu_corporum_in_gyrum" title="De motu corporum in gyrum">De motu corporum in gyrum</a> ('On the motion of bodies in an orbit')</i>, which provided a physical justification for <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">Kepler's laws of planetary motion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-21">[21]</a></sup> Halley was impressed by the manuscript and urged Newton to expand on it, and a few years later Newton published a groundbreaking book called <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica</a></i> (<i>Mathematical Principles of Natural Philosophy</i>). In this book, Newton described gravitation as a universal force, and claimed  that "the forces which keep the planets in their orbs must [be] reciprocally as the squares of their distances from the centers about which they revolve." This statement was later condensed into the following inverse-square law:
</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F=G{\frac {m_{1}m_{2}}{r^{2}}},}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>G</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<msub>
<mi>m</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msub>
<mi>m</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mrow>
<msup>
<mi>r</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F=G{\frac {m_{1}m_{2}}{r^{2}}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle F=G{\frac {m_{1}m_{2}}{r^{2}}},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/48f74b3b4d591ba1996c4d481f74ac3ab7e279d7" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:14.338ex; height:5.009ex;"/></div>where <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">F</span> is the force, <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i><sub>1</sub></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i><sub>2</sub></span> are the masses of the objects interacting, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">r</span> is the distance between the centers of the masses and <span class="texhtml"><i>G</i></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">gravitational constant</a> <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6989667400000000000♠"></span>6.674<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−11</sup> m<sup>3</sup>⋅kg<sup>−1</sup>⋅s<sup>−2</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physconst-G_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-physconst-G-22">[22]</a></sup>.
</p><p>Newton's <i>Principia</i> was well-received by the scientific community, and his law of gravitation quickly spread across the European world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> More than a century later, in 1821, his theory of gravitation rose to even greater prominence when it was used to predict the existence of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>. In that year, the French astronomer <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Bouvard" title="Alexis Bouvard">Alexis Bouvard</a> used this theory to create a table modeling the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, which was shown to differ significantly from the planet's actual trajectory. In order to explain this discrepancy, many astronomers speculated that there might be a large object beyond the orbit of Uranus which was disrupting its orbit. In 1846, the astronomers <a href="/wiki/John_Couch_Adams" title="John Couch Adams">John Couch Adams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urbain_Le_Verrier" title="Urbain Le Verrier">Urbain Le Verrier</a> independently used Newton's law to predict Neptune's location in the night sky, and the planet was discovered there within a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_relativity">General relativity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Introduction_to_general_relativity" title="Introduction to general relativity">Introduction to general relativity</a></div>

<ul><li><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="530" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg/14px-Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg/21px-Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg/28px-Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg.png 2x" width="14"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Physics" title="Portal:Physics">Physics portal</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/16px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/23px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/31px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Category:General_relativity" title="Category:General relativity">Category</a></span></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:General_relativity_sidebar" title="Template:General relativity sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:General_relativity_sidebar" title="Template talk:General relativity sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:General_relativity_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Eventually, astronomers noticed an eccentricity in the orbit of the planet <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> which could not be explained by Newton's theory: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a> of the orbit was increasing by about 42.98 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcseconds" title="Arcseconds">arcseconds</a> per century. The most obvious explanation for this discrepancy was an as-yet-undiscovered celestial body (such as a planet orbiting the Sun even closer than Mercury), but all efforts to find such a body turned out to be fruitless. Finally, in 1915, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> developed a theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> which was able to accurately model Mercury's orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>In general relativity, the effects of gravitation are ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a> <a href="/wiki/Curvature" title="Curvature">curvature</a> instead of a force. Einstein began to toy with this idea in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">equivalence principle</a>, a discovery which he later described as "the happiest thought of my life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In this theory, free fall is considered to be equivalent to inertial motion, meaning that free-falling inertial objects are accelerated relative to non-inertial observers on the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In contrast to <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">Newtonian physics</a>, Einstein believed that it was possible for this acceleration to occur without any force being applied to the object.
</p><p>Einstein proposed that spacetime is curved by matter, and that free-falling objects are moving along locally straight paths in curved spacetime. These straight paths are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geodesic_(general_relativity)" title="Geodesic (general relativity)">geodesics</a>. As in Newton's first law of motion, Einstein believed that a force applied to an object would cause it to deviate from a geodesic. For instance, people standing on the surface of the Earth are prevented from following a geodesic path because the mechanical resistance of the Earth exerts an upward force on them. This explains why moving along the geodesics in spacetime is considered inertial.
</p><p>Einstein's description of gravity was quickly accepted by the majority of physicists, as it was able to explain a wide variety of previously baffling experimental results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In the coming years, a wide range of experiments provided additional support for the idea of general relativity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Today, Einstein's theory of relativity is used for all gravitational calculations where absolute precision is desired, although Newton's inverse-square law continues to be a useful and fairly accurate approximation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_research">Modern research</span></h2>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_physics" title="Modern physics">modern physics</a>, general relativity remains the framework for the understanding of gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Physicists continue to work to find <a href="/wiki/Solutions_of_the_Einstein_field_equations" title="Solutions of the Einstein field equations">solutions</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Einstein_field_equations" title="Einstein field equations">Einstein field equations</a> that form the basis of general relativity, while some scientists have speculated that general relativity may not be applicable at all in certain scenarios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Einstein_field_equations">Einstein field equations</span></h3>
<p>The Einstein field equations are a <a href="/wiki/System_of_equations" title="System of equations">system</a> of 10 <a href="/wiki/Partial_differential_equation" title="Partial differential equation">partial differential equations</a> which describe how matter affects the curvature of spacetime. The system is often expressed in the following simplified form:

</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schwarzchild-metric.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="238" data-file-width="461" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Schwarzchild-metric.jpg/300px-Schwarzchild-metric.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Schwarzchild-metric.jpg/450px-Schwarzchild-metric.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Schwarzchild-metric.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schwarzchild-metric.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of the Schwarzchild metric, which describes spacetime around a spherical, uncharged, and nonrotating object with mass</div></div></div>
<p>A major area of research is the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Exact_solutions_in_general_relativity" title="Exact solutions in general relativity">exact solutions</a> to the Einstein field equations. Solving these equations amounts to calculating a precise value for the metric tensor (which defines the curvature and geometry of spacetime) under certain physical conditions. There is no formal definition for what constitutes such solutions, but most scientists agree that they should be expressable using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elementary_functions" title="Elementary functions">elementary functions</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_differential_equations" title="Linear differential equations">linear differential equations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Some of the most notable solutions of the equations include: 
</p>
<ul><li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schwarzschild_solution" title="Schwarzschild solution">Schwarzschild solution</a>, which describes spacetime surrounding a <a href="/wiki/Circular_symmetry" title="Circular symmetry">spherically symmetric</a> non-<a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">rotating</a> uncharged massive object. For compact enough objects, this solution generated a <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a> with a central <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_singularity" title="Gravitational singularity">singularity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> At points far away from the central mass, the accelerations predicted by the Schwarzschild solution are practically identical to those predicted by Newton's theory of gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Reissner%E2%80%93Nordstr%C3%B6m_metric" title="Reissner–Nordström metric">Reissner-Nordström solution</a>, which analyzes a non-rotating spherically symmetric object with charge and was independently discovered by several different researchers between 1916 and 1921.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> In some cases, this solution can predict the existence of black holes with double <a href="/wiki/Event_horizon" title="Event horizon">event horizons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Kerr_metric" title="Kerr metric">Kerr solution</a>, which generalizes the Schwarzchild solution to rotating massive objects. Because of the difficulty of factoring in the effects of rotation into Einstein's field equations, this solution was not discovered until 1963.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Kerr%E2%80%93Newman_metric" title="Kerr–Newman metric">Kerr-Newman solution</a> for charged, rotating massive objects. This solution was derived in 1964, using the same technique of complex coordinate transformation that was used for the Kerr solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">cosmological</a> <a href="/wiki/Friedmann%E2%80%93Lema%C3%AEtre%E2%80%93Robertson%E2%80%93Walker_metric" title="Friedmann–Lemaître–Robertson–Walker metric">Friedmann-Lemaître-Robertson-Walker solution</a>, discovered in 1922 by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Friedmann" title="Alexander Friedmann">Alexander Friedmann</a> and then confirmed in 1927 by <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lemaître">Georges Lemaître</a>. This solution was revolutionary for predicting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expansion_of_the_Universe" title="Expansion of the Universe">expansion of the Universe</a>, which was confirmed seven years later after a series of measurements by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> It even showed that general relativity was incompatible with a <a href="/wiki/Static_universe" title="Static universe">static universe</a>, and Einstein later conceded that he had been wrong to design his field equations to account for a Universe that was not expanding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Today, there remain many important situations in which the Einstein field equations have not been solved. Chief among these is the <a href="/wiki/Two-body_problem" title="Two-body problem">two-body problem</a>, which concerns the geometry of spacetime around two mutually interacting massive objects (such as the Sun and the Earth, or the two stars in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_star_system" title="Binary star system">binary star system</a>). The situation gets even more complicated when considering the interactions of three or more massive bodies (the "n-body problem"), and some scientists suspect that the Einstein field equations will never be solved in this context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> However, it is still possible to construct an approximate solution to the field equations in the n-body problem by using the technique of <a href="/wiki/Post-Newtonian_expansion" title="Post-Newtonian expansion">post-Newtonian expansion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> In general, the extreme nonlinearity of Einstein's field equations makes it difficult to solve them in all but the most specific cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravity_and_quantum_mechanics">Gravity and quantum mechanics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Graviton" title="Graviton">Graviton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">Quantum gravity</a></div>
<p>Despite its success in predicting the effects of gravity at large scales, general relativity is ultimately incompatible with <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>. This is because general relativity describes gravity as a smooth, continuous distortion of spacetime, while quantum mechanics holds that all forces arise from the exchange of discrete particles known as <a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum">quanta</a>. This contradiction is especially vexing to physicists because the other three fundamental forces (strong force, weak force and electromagnetism) were reconciled with a quantum framework decades ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> As a result, modern researchers have begun to search for a theory that could unite both gravity and quantum mechanics under a more general framework.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>One path is to describe gravity in the framework of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a>, which has been successful to accurately describe the other <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">fundamental interactions</a>. The electromagnetic force arises from an exchange of virtual <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>, where the QFT description of gravity is that there is an exchange of <a href="/wiki/Virtual_particle" title="Virtual particle">virtual</a> <a href="/wiki/Graviton" title="Graviton">gravitons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> This description reproduces general relativity in the <a href="/wiki/Classical_limit" title="Classical limit">classical limit</a>. However, this approach fails at short distances of the order of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck_length" title="Planck length">Planck length</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Randall,_Lisa_2005_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Randall,_Lisa_2005-54">[54]</a></sup> where a more complete theory of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">quantum gravity</a> (or a new approach to quantum mechanics) is required.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tests_of_general_relativity">Tests of general relativity</span></h3>
<p>Testing the predictions of general relativity has historically been difficult, because they are almost identical to the predictions of Newtonian gravity for small energies and masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-55">[55]</a></sup> Still, since its development, an ongoing series of experimental results have provided support for the theory:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1919_eclipse_positive.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="899" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/1919_eclipse_positive.jpg/220px-1919_eclipse_positive.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/1919_eclipse_positive.jpg/330px-1919_eclipse_positive.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/1919_eclipse_positive.jpg/440px-1919_eclipse_positive.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1919_eclipse_positive.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1919 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Total_solar_eclipse" title="Total solar eclipse">total solar eclipse</a> provided one of the first opportunities to test the predictions of general relativity</div></div></div>
<ul><li>In 1919, the British astrophysicist <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Eddington" title="Arthur Eddington">Arthur Eddington</a> was able to confirm the predicted <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" title="Gravitational lens">gravitational lensing</a> of light during <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_May_29,_1919" title="Solar eclipse of May 29, 1919">that year's solar eclipse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Eddington measured starlight deflections twice those predicted by Newtonian corpuscular theory, in accordance with the predictions of general relativity. Although Eddington's analysis was later disputed, this experiment made Einstein famous almost overnight and caused general relativity to become widely accepted in the scientific community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1959, American physicists <a href="/wiki/Robert_Pound" title="Robert Pound">Robert Pound</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glen_Rebka" title="Glen Rebka">Glen Rebka</a> performed <a href="/wiki/Pound%E2%80%93Rebka_experiment" title="Pound–Rebka experiment">an experiment</a> in which they used <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> to confirm the prediction of <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_time_dilation" title="Gravitational time dilation">gravitational time dilation</a>. By sending the rays down a 74-foot tower and measuring their frequency at the bottom, the scientists confirmed that light is <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshifted</a> as it moves towards a source of gravity. The observed redshift also supported the idea that time runs more slowly in the presence of a gravitational field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_delay_of_light" title="Time delay of light">time delay of light</a> passing close to a massive object was first identified by <a href="/wiki/Irwin_I._Shapiro" title="Irwin I. Shapiro">Irwin I. Shapiro</a> in 1964 in interplanetary spacecraft signals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1971, scientists discovered the first-ever black hole in the galaxy <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_A" title="Cygnus A">Cygnus</a>. The black hole was detected because it was emitting bursts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-rays" title="X-rays">x-rays</a> as it consumed a smaller star, and it came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_X-1" title="Cygnus X-1">Cygnus X-1</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> This discovery confirmed yet another prediction of general relativity, because Einstein's equations implied that light could not escape from a sufficiently large and compact object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></li>
<li>General relativity states that gravity acts on light and matter equally, meaning that a sufficiently massive object could warp light around it and create a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_lensing" title="Gravitational lensing">gravitational lens</a>. This phenomenon was first confirmed by observation in 1979 using the 2.1 meter telescope at <a href="/wiki/Kitt_Peak_National_Observatory" title="Kitt Peak National Observatory">Kitt Peak National Observatory</a> in Arizona, which saw two mirror images of the same quasar whose light had been bent around the galaxy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/YGKOW_G1" title="YGKOW G1">YGKOW G1</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frame_dragging" title="Frame dragging">Frame dragging</a>, the idea that a rotating massive object should twist spacetime around it, was confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Gravity_Probe_B" title="Gravity Probe B">Gravity Probe B</a> results in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/LIGO" title="LIGO">LIGO</a> observatory detected faint <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_waves" title="Gravitational waves">gravitational waves</a>, the existence of which had been predicted by general relativity. Scientists believe that the waves emanated from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_hole_merger" title="Black hole merger">black hole merger</a> that occurred 1.5 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light-years" title="Light-years">light-years</a> away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specifics">Specifics</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Earth.27s_gravity"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth's_gravity">Earth's gravity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Falling_ball.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2751" data-file-width="819" decoding="async" height="336" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Falling_ball.jpg/100px-Falling_ball.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Falling_ball.jpg/150px-Falling_ball.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Falling_ball.jpg/200px-Falling_ball.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Falling_ball.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An initially-stationary object that is allowed to fall freely under gravity drops a distance that is proportional to the square of the elapsed time. This image spans half a second and was captured at 20 flashes per second.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">Gravity of Earth</a></div>
<p>Every planetary body (including the Earth) is surrounded by its own gravitational field, which can be conceptualized with Newtonian physics as exerting an attractive force on all objects. Assuming a spherically symmetrical planet, the strength of this field at any given point above the surface is proportional to the planetary body's mass and inversely proportional to the square of the distance from the center of the body.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gravity_action-reaction.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="647" decoding="async" height="43" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Gravity_action-reaction.gif/150px-Gravity_action-reaction.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Gravity_action-reaction.gif/225px-Gravity_action-reaction.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Gravity_action-reaction.gif/300px-Gravity_action-reaction.gif 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gravity_action-reaction.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>If an object with comparable mass to that of the Earth were to fall towards it, then the corresponding acceleration of the Earth would be observable.</div></div></div>
<p>The strength of the gravitational field is numerically equal to the acceleration of objects under its influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The rate of acceleration of falling objects near the Earth's surface varies very slightly depending on latitude, surface features such as mountains and ridges, and perhaps unusually high or low sub-surface densities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> For purposes of weights and measures, a <a href="/wiki/Standard_gravity" title="Standard gravity">standard gravity</a> value is defined by the <a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a>, under the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a> (SI).
</p><p>The force of gravity on Earth is the resultant (vector sum) of two forces:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> (a) The gravitational attraction in accordance with Newton's universal law of gravitation, and (b) the centrifugal force, which results from the choice of an earthbound, rotating frame of reference. The force of gravity is weakest at the equator because of the <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force" title="Centrifugal force">centrifugal force</a> caused by the Earth's rotation and because points on the equator are furthest from the center of the Earth. The force of gravity varies with latitude and increases from about 9.780 m/s<sup>2</sup> at the Equator to about 9.832 m/s<sup>2</sup> at the poles.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Canada's <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a> has less gravity than any place on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span></h3>
<p>The earliest gravity (possibly in the form of quantum gravity, <a href="/wiki/Supergravity" title="Supergravity">supergravity</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_singularity" title="Gravitational singularity">gravitational singularity</a>), along with ordinary space and time, developed during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_formation_of_the_Universe#Planck_Epoch" title="Timeline of the formation of the Universe">Planck epoch</a> (up to 10<sup>−43</sup> seconds after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">birth</a> of the Universe), possibly from a primeval state (such as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/False_vacuum" title="False vacuum">false vacuum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_vacuum" title="Quantum vacuum">quantum vacuum</a> or <a href="/wiki/Virtual_particle" title="Virtual particle">virtual particle</a>), in a currently unknown manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck-UOregon_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Planck-UOregon-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_radiation">Gravitational radiation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LIGO_Hanford_aerial_05.jpg"><img alt="LIGO Hanford Observatory" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/LIGO_Hanford_aerial_05.jpg/220px-LIGO_Hanford_aerial_05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/LIGO_Hanford_aerial_05.jpg/330px-LIGO_Hanford_aerial_05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/LIGO_Hanford_aerial_05.jpg/440px-LIGO_Hanford_aerial_05.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LIGO_Hanford_aerial_05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/LIGO" title="LIGO">LIGO</a> Hanford Observatory located in Washington, United States, where gravitational waves were first observed in September 2015.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">Gravitational wave</a></div>
<p>General relativity predicts that energy can be transported out of a system through gravitational radiation. The first indirect evidence for gravitational radiation was through measurements of the <a href="/wiki/Hulse%E2%80%93Taylor_binary" title="Hulse–Taylor binary">Hulse–Taylor binary</a> in 1973. This system consists of a pulsar and neutron star in orbit around one another. Its orbital period has decreased since its initial discovery due to a loss of energy, which is consistent for the amount of energy loss due to gravitational radiation. This research was awarded the Nobel Prize in Physics in 1993.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The first direct evidence for gravitational radiation was measured on 14 September 2015 by the <a href="/wiki/LIGO" title="LIGO">LIGO</a> detectors. The gravitational waves emitted during the collision of two black holes 1.3 billion-light years from Earth were measured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark_2016_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clark_2016-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discovery_2016_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Discovery_2016-73">[73]</a></sup> This observation confirms the theoretical predictions of Einstein and others that such waves exist. It also opens the way for practical observation and understanding of the nature of gravity and events in the Universe including the Big Bang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">Neutron star</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a> formation also create detectable amounts of gravitational radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysRev2017_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-PhysRev2017-75">[75]</a></sup> This research was awarded the Nobel Prize in physics in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Speed_of_gravity">Speed of gravity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_gravity" title="Speed of gravity">Speed of gravity</a></div>
<p>In December 2012, a research team in China announced that it had produced measurements of the phase lag of <a href="/wiki/Earth_tide" title="Earth tide">Earth tides</a> during full and new moons which seem to prove that the speed of gravity is equal to the speed of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> This means that if the Sun suddenly disappeared, the Earth would keep orbiting the vacant point normally for 8 minutes, which is the time light takes to travel that distance. The team's findings were released in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Science_Bulletin" title="Chinese Science Bulletin">Chinese Science Bulletin</a> in February 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2017, the <a href="/wiki/LIGO" title="LIGO">LIGO</a> and Virgo detectors received gravitational wave signals within 2 seconds of gamma ray satellites and optical telescopes seeing signals from the same direction. This confirmed that the speed of gravitational waves was the same as the speed of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anomalies_and_discrepancies">Anomalies and discrepancies</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Gravity_anomaly" title="Gravity anomaly">Gravity anomaly</a>.</div>
<p>There are some observations that are not adequately accounted for, which may point to the need for better theories of gravity or perhaps be explained in other ways.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GalacticRotation2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="150" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/GalacticRotation2.svg/250px-GalacticRotation2.svg.png" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption">Rotation curve of a typical spiral galaxy: predicted (<b>A</b>) and observed (<b>B</b>). The discrepancy between the curves is attributed to <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><b>Extra-fast stars</b>: Stars in galaxies follow a <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_rotation_curve" title="Galaxy rotation curve">distribution of velocities</a> where stars on the outskirts are moving faster than they should according to the observed distributions of normal matter. Galaxies within <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_groups_and_clusters" title="Galaxy groups and clusters">galaxy clusters</a> show a similar pattern. <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">Dark matter</a>, which would interact through gravitation but not electromagnetically, would account for the discrepancy. Various <a href="/wiki/Modified_Newtonian_dynamics" title="Modified Newtonian dynamics">modifications to Newtonian dynamics</a> have also been proposed.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Flyby_anomaly" title="Flyby anomaly">Flyby anomaly</a></b>: Various spacecraft have experienced greater acceleration than expected during <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a> maneuvers.</li>
<li><b>Accelerating expansion</b>: The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">metric expansion of space</a> seems to be speeding up. <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">Dark energy</a> has been proposed to explain this. A recent alternative explanation is that the geometry of space is not homogeneous (due to clusters of galaxies) and that when the data are reinterpreted to take this into account, the expansion is not speeding up after all,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> however this conclusion is disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Anomalous increase of the <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical unit</a></b>: Recent measurements indicate that <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit#Developments" title="Astronomical unit">planetary orbits are widening</a> faster than if this were solely through the Sun losing mass by radiating energy.</li>
<li><b>Extra energetic photons</b>: Photons travelling through galaxy clusters should gain energy and then lose it again on the way out. The accelerating expansion of the Universe should stop the photons returning all the energy, but even taking this into account photons from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">cosmic microwave background radiation</a> gain twice as much energy as expected. This may indicate that gravity falls off faster than inverse-squared at certain distance scales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newsci2699_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-newsci2699-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Extra massive hydrogen clouds</b>: The spectral lines of the <a href="/wiki/Lyman-alpha_forest" title="Lyman-alpha forest">Lyman-alpha forest</a> suggest that hydrogen clouds are more clumped together at certain scales than expected and, like <a href="/wiki/Dark_flow" title="Dark flow">dark flow</a>, may indicate that gravity falls off slower than inverse-squared at certain distance scales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newsci2699_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-newsci2699-82">[82]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_theories">Alternative theories</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alternatives_to_general_relativity" title="Alternatives to general relativity">Alternatives to general relativity</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_alternative_theories">Historical alternative theories</span></h3>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristotelian_theory_of_gravity" title="Aristotelian theory of gravity">Aristotelian theory of gravity</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Le_Sage%27s_theory_of_gravitation" title="Le Sage's theory of gravitation">Le Sage's theory of gravitation</a> (1784) also called LeSage gravity but originally proposed by Fatio and further elaborated by <a href="/wiki/Georges-Louis_Le_Sage" title="Georges-Louis Le Sage">Georges-Louis Le Sage</a>, based on a fluid-based explanation where a light gas fills the entire Universe.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ritz%27s_Equation" title="Ritz's Equation">Ritz's theory of gravitation</a>, <i>Ann. Chem. Phys.</i> 13, 145, (1908) pp. 267–271, Weber-Gauss electrodynamics applied to gravitation. Classical advancement of perihelia.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nordstr%C3%B6m%27s_theory_of_gravitation" title="Nordström's theory of gravitation">Nordström's theory of gravitation</a> (1912, 1913), an early competitor of general relativity.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kaluza%E2%80%93Klein_theory" title="Kaluza–Klein theory">Kaluza Klein theory</a> (1921)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Whitehead%27s_theory_of_gravitation" title="Whitehead's theory of gravitation">Whitehead's theory of gravitation</a> (1922), another early competitor of general relativity.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_alternative_theories">Modern alternative theories</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Brans%E2%80%93Dicke_theory" title="Brans–Dicke theory">Brans–Dicke theory</a> of gravity (1961)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2014Schpj...931358B_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-2014Schpj...931358B-83">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Induced_gravity" title="Induced gravity">Induced gravity</a> (1967), a proposal by <a href="/wiki/Andrei_Sakharov" title="Andrei Sakharov">Andrei Sakharov</a> according to which <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> might arise from <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theories</a> of matter</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">String theory</a> (late 1960s)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/F(R)_gravity" title="F(R) gravity">ƒ(R) gravity</a> (1970)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Horndeski%27s_theory" title="Horndeski's theory">Horndeski theory</a> (1974)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1974IJTP...10..363H_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-1974IJTP...10..363H-84">[84]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Supergravity" title="Supergravity">Supergravity</a> (1976)</li>
<li>In the <a href="/wiki/Modified_Newtonian_dynamics" title="Modified Newtonian dynamics">modified Newtonian dynamics</a> (MOND) (1981), <a href="/wiki/Mordehai_Milgrom" title="Mordehai Milgrom">Mordehai Milgrom</a> proposes a modification of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_second_law" title="Newton's second law">Newton's second law</a> of motion for small accelerations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2014SchpJ...931410M_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-2014SchpJ...931410M-85">[85]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Self-creation_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Self-creation cosmology (page does not exist)">self-creation cosmology</a> theory of gravity (1982) by G.A. Barber in which the Brans-Dicke theory is modified to allow mass creation</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Loop_quantum_gravity" title="Loop quantum gravity">Loop quantum gravity</a> (1988) by <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Rovelli" title="Carlo Rovelli">Carlo Rovelli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lee_Smolin" title="Lee Smolin">Lee Smolin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abhay_Ashtekar" title="Abhay Ashtekar">Abhay Ashtekar</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nonsymmetric_gravitational_theory" title="Nonsymmetric gravitational theory">Nonsymmetric gravitational theory</a> (NGT) (1994) by <a href="/wiki/John_Moffat_(physicist)" title="John Moffat (physicist)">John Moffat</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tensor%E2%80%93vector%E2%80%93scalar_gravity" title="Tensor–vector–scalar gravity">Tensor–vector–scalar gravity</a> (TeVeS) (2004), a relativistic modification of MOND by <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Bekenstein" title="Jacob Bekenstein">Jacob Bekenstein</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chameleon_particle" title="Chameleon particle">Chameleon theory</a> (2004) by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Justin_Khoury&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Justin Khoury (page does not exist)">Justin Khoury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amanda_Weltman" title="Amanda Weltman">Amanda Weltman</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pressuron" title="Pressuron">Pressuron theory</a> (2013) by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Olivier_Minazzoli&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Olivier Minazzoli (page does not exist)">Olivier Minazzoli</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aur%C3%A9lien_Hees&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aurélien Hees (page does not exist)">Aurélien Hees</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Conformal_gravity" title="Conformal gravity">Conformal gravity</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravity_as_an_entropic_force" title="Gravity as an entropic force">Gravity as an entropic force</a>, gravity arising as an emergent phenomenon from the thermodynamic concept of entropy.</li>
<li>In the <a href="/wiki/Superfluid_vacuum_theory" title="Superfluid vacuum theory">superfluid vacuum theory</a> the gravity and curved space-time arise as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collective_excitation" title="Collective excitation">collective excitation</a> mode of non-relativistic background <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superfluid" title="Superfluid">superfluid</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Massive_gravity" title="Massive gravity">Massive gravity</a>, a theory where gravitons and gravitational waves have a non-zero mass</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 21em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Anti-gravity" title="Anti-gravity">Anti-gravity</a>, the idea of neutralizing or repelling gravity</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Artificial_gravity" title="Artificial gravity">Artificial gravity</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gauss%27s_law_for_gravity" title="Gauss's law for gravity">Gauss's law for gravity</a></li>

<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo" title="Galileo">Galileo</a> (1638), <i><a href="/wiki/Two_New_Sciences" title="Two New Sciences">Two New Sciences</a></i>, First Day Salviati speaks: "If this were what Aristotle meant you would burden him with another error which would amount to a falsehood; because, since there is no such sheer height available on earth, it is clear that Aristotle could not have made the experiment; yet he wishes to give us the impression of his having performed it when he speaks of such an effect as one which we see."</span>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">1 July</span> 2019</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Phil.+Trans.+Roy.+Soc.+A&amp;rft.atitle=A+Determination+of+the+Deflection+of+Light+by+the+Sun%27s+Gravitational+Field%2C+from+Observations+Made+at+the+Total+Eclipse+of+May+29%2C+1919&amp;rft.volume=220&amp;rft.issue=571%E2%80%93581&amp;rft.pages=291-333&amp;rft.date=1920&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1098%2Frsta.1920.0009&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1920RSPTA.220..291D&amp;rft.aulast=Dyson&amp;rft.aufirst=F.W.&amp;rft.au=Eddington%2C+A.S.&amp;rft.au=Davidson%2C+C.R.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fzenodo.org%2Frecord%2F1432106&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGravity"></span>. Quote, p. 332: "Thus the results of the expeditions to Sobral and Principe can leave little doubt that a deflection of light takes place in the neighbourhood of the sun and that it is of the amount demanded by Einstein's generalised theory of relativity, as attributable to the sun's gravitational field."</span>
